# 2019â€“2020 United States blizzard

The 2019â€“2020 United States blizzard was a major winter storm that affected the Midwestern and Northeastern United States from January 28 to February 1, 2020. The storm produced heavy snow, strong winds, and blizzard conditions in the Midwest, and heavy rain and flooding in the Northeast. The storm was the first major winter storm of the 2019â€“2020 North American winter.

## Meteorological history

On January 27, the Storm Prediction Center (SPC) outlined a slight risk for severe weather across portions of the Southern United States, with a marginal risk for severe weather extending northward into the Ohio Valley and Mid-Atlantic. The next day, the SPC expanded the slight risk northward into the Ohio Valley and Mid-Atlantic, with a marginal risk for severe weather extending into the Northeast.

On January 29, the SPC expanded the slight risk for severe weather into the Northeast, with a marginal risk for severe weather extending into Atlantic Canada. The next day, the SPC expanded the slight risk for severe weather into the Northeast, with a marginal risk for severe weather extending into Atlantic Canada.

## Preparations

On January 28, the National Weather Service (NWS) issued a winter storm watch for portions of the Midwest. The next day, the NWS expanded the winter storm watch into the Northeast. On January 30, the NWS issued a blizzard warning for portions of the Midwest, with a winter storm warning for portions of the Northeast.

## Impact

The storm produced heavy snow, strong winds, and blizzard conditions in the Midwest, and heavy rain and flooding in the Northeast.

## Midwest

In the Midwest, the storm produced heavy snow, strong winds, and blizzard conditions. The storm caused widespread power outages, with over 100,000 customers losing power. The storm also caused numerous traffic accidents, with over 100 people being injured.

## Northeast

In the Northeast, the storm produced heavy rain and flooding. The storm caused widespread power outages, with over 100,000 customers losing power. The storm also caused numerous traffic accidents, with over 100 people being injured.

## Aftermath

In the Midwest, the storm caused widespread damage, with over $100 million in damages. In the Northeast, the storm caused widespread damage, with over $100 million in damages.

# 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests

On 12 November, the Hong Kong government announced that it would ban face masks in public assemblies, citing the need to maintain public order. The ban was met with widespread condemnation from the international community, including the United States, the United Kingdom, and the European Union. The ban was also criticised by the United Nations High Commissioner for Human Rights, Michelle Bachelet, who said that it "raises serious concerns about the right to freedom of expression and peaceful assembly".

On 18 November, the Hong Kong government announced that it would postpone the implementation of the mask ban, citing the need to "cool down the situation". The ban was eventually implemented on 4 October 2020.

## 2020

On 1 January 2020, thousands of protesters gathered in Victoria Park to mark the start of the new year and the first anniversary of the protests. The protest was largely peaceful, with protesters chanting slogans and waving flags.

On 1 February 2020, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be introducing a new national security law. The law was met with widespread opposition from the protesters, who saw it as a threat to their freedoms.

On 27 June 2020, the Hong Kong government passed the national security law. The law criminalises acts of secession, subversion, terrorism, and collusion with foreign forces. The law has been widely criticised by the international community, including the United States, the United Kingdom, and the European Union.

On 1 August 2020, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be banning the display of the British colonial flag and the colonial emblem. The ban was met with widespread condemnation from the protesters, who saw it as a further erosion of their freedoms.

On 12 August 2020, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be banning the display of the colonial flag and emblem in public places. The ban was met with widespread condemnation from the protesters, who saw it as a further erosion of their freedoms.

On 1 October 2020, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be banning the display of the colonial flag and emblem in public places. The ban was met with widespread condemnation from the protesters, who saw it as a further erosion of their freedoms.

On 1 November 2020, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be banning the display of the colonial flag and emblem in public places. The ban was met with widespread condemnation from the protesters, who saw it as a further erosion of their freedoms.

On 1 December 2020, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be banning the display of the colonial flag and emblem in public places. The ban was met with widespread condemnation from the protesters, who saw it as a further erosion of their freedoms.

On 1 January 2021, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be banning the display of the colonial flag and emblem in public places. The ban was met with widespread condemnation from the protesters, who saw it as a further erosion of their freedoms.

## Aftermath

The 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests had a profound impact on Hong Kong society. The protests led to a decline in tourism, a drop in retail sales, and a decrease in foreign investment. The protests also had a chilling effect on freedom of expression and assembly in Hong Kong.

The protests also had a significant impact on the relationship between Hong Kong and China. The Chinese government saw the protests as a threat to its sovereignty and stability. In response, the Chinese government passed a national security law in Hong Kong. The law has been widely criticised by the international community, including the United States, the United Kingdom, and the European Union.

The 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests were a watershed moment in Hong Kong's history. They demonstrated the resilience of the people of Hong Kong and their commitment to democracy and freedom. The protests also highlighted the deep divisions that exist in Hong Kong society.

# 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests

The 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests were a series of pro-democracy demonstrations and civil unrest that took place in Hong Kong from 2019 to 2020. The protests were sparked by the introduction of the Fugitive Offenders and Mutual Legal Assistance in Criminal Matters Legislation (Amendment) Bill 2019, which would have allowed the transfer of fugitives to mainland China for trial. The bill was widely seen as a threat to the "one country, two systems" principle and the city's judicial independence.

The protests began on 9 June 2019, with a peaceful march that attracted over a million participants, the largest demonstration in Hong Kong's history. The protests escalated into a series of increasingly violent confrontations between protesters and the police, with protesters occupying major roads and public spaces, and the police responding with tear gas, rubber bullets, and water cannons. The protests were marked by a high level of public participation, with many ordinary citizens joining the demonstrations, and a significant degree of international attention and support.

The protests were also notable for their use of innovative tactics, such as the use of laser pointers to disorient police officers and the creation of "flash mobs" to evade police surveillance. The protests were also marked by a high degree of organization and coordination, with protesters using social media platforms such as Telegram and LIHKG to plan and communicate their actions.

The protests were met with a variety of responses from the Hong Kong government and the Chinese government. The Hong Kong government initially responded with a hardline stance, refusing to meet the protesters' demands and accusing them of being rioters. The Chinese government also took a hardline stance, accusing the protesters of being separatists and terrorists.

Despite the protests, the Fugitive Offenders and Mutual Legal Assistance in Criminal Matters Legislation (Amendment) Bill 2019 was passed by the Hong Kong Legislative Council on 18 June 2019. The bill was signed into law by Hong Kong Chief Executive Carrie Lam on 30 June 2019.

The protests continued into 2020, with a series of smaller demonstrations and civil disobedience actions taking place throughout the year. The protests were eventually suppressed by the Hong Kong government, with the help of the Chinese government, in the form of a national security law that was passed in June 2020. The law criminalized a number of activities, including secession, subversion, terrorism, and collusion with foreign forces, and gave the government broad powers to suppress dissent.

The 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests were a significant event in the history of Hong Kong, and they had a profound impact on the city's politics and society. The protests were a testament to the resilience and determination of the people of Hong Kong, and they highlighted the importance of the "one country, two systems" principle.

## Background

The protests were sparked by the introduction of the Fugitive Offenders and Mutual Legal Assistance in Criminal Matters Legislation (Amendment) Bill 2019, which would have allowed the transfer of fugitives to mainland China for trial. The bill was widely seen as a threat to the "one country, two systems" principle and the city's judicial independence.

The protests were also fueled by a number of other grievances, including:

* The erosion of civil liberties and freedoms in Hong Kong.

* The high cost of living in Hong Kong.

* The lack of affordable housing.

* The perceived corruption of the Hong Kong government.

* The lack of democracy in Hong Kong.

The protests were a continuation of a series of pro-democracy protests that had been taking place in Hong Kong since the Umbrella Revolution in 2014. The Umbrella Revolution was a series of protests that were also sparked by the perceived erosion of civil liberties and freedoms in Hong Kong.

## Timeline

The protests began on 9 June 2019, with a peaceful march that attracted over a million participants, the largest demonstration in Hong Kong's history. The protests escalated into a series of increasingly violent confrontations between protesters and the police, with protesters occupying major roads and public spaces, and the police responding with tear gas, rubber bullets, and water cannons.

The protests were marked by a high level of public participation, with many ordinary citizens joining the demonstrations, and a significant degree of international attention and support. The protests were also notable for their use of innovative tactics, such as the use of laser pointers to disorient police officers and the creation of "flash mobs" to evade police surveillance. The protests were also marked by a high degree of organization and coordination, with protesters using social media platforms such as Telegram and LIHKG to plan and communicate their actions.

The protests were met with a variety of responses from the Hong Kong government and the Chinese government. The Hong Kong government initially responded with a hardline stance, refusing to meet the protesters' demands and accusing them of being rioters. The Chinese government also took a hardline stance, accusing the protesters of being separatists and terrorists.

Despite the protests, the Fugitive Offenders and Mutual Legal Assistance in Criminal Matters Legislation (Amendment) Bill 2019 was passed by the Hong Kong Legislative Council on 18 June 2019. The bill was signed into law by Hong Kong Chief Executive Carrie Lam on 30 June 2019.

The protests continued into 2020, with a series of smaller demonstrations and civil disobedience actions taking place throughout the year. The protests were eventually suppressed by the Hong Kong government, with the help of the Chinese government, in the form of a national security law that was passed in June 2020. The law criminalized a number of activities, including secession, subversion, terrorism, and collusion with foreign forces, and gave the government broad powers to suppress dissent.

## Impact

The 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests were a significant event in the history of Hong Kong, and they had a profound impact on the city's politics and society. The protests were a testament to the resilience and determination of the people of Hong Kong, and they highlighted the importance of the "one country, two systems" principle.

The protests also had a number of other impacts, including:

* The erosion of trust between the Hong Kong government and the people of Hong Kong.

* The increase in political polarization in Hong Kong.

* The decline of Hong Kong's international reputation.

* The impact on Hong Kong's economy.

The protests were a reminder of the importance of democracy and civil liberties, and they showed that the people of Hong Kong are willing to fight for their rights. The protests also showed that the "one country, two systems" principle is under threat, and that the Chinese government is willing to use force to suppress dissent.

# 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests

On 12 November, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be suspending the extradition bill. The announcement was made after a meeting of the Executive Council, with Chief Executive Carrie Lam saying that the government would "not proceed with the legislative amendment exercise". Lam also said that the government would "actively engage with different sectors of the community and all sectors of the community to enhance communication and mutual understanding".

## 12 November march

On 12 November, a march was held in Hong Kong to celebrate the suspension of the extradition bill. The march was organised by the Civil Human Rights Front, and was the first march to be held in Hong Kong since the 1 September march. The march was attended by an estimated 180,000 people, according to the police, and 550,000 people, according to the organisers.

# 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests

The 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests were a series of pro-democracy demonstrations and civil resistance in Hong Kong that began in late 2019 and continued into 2020. The protests were sparked by the now-withdrawn extradition bill, which would have allowed the transfer of fugitives to mainland China for trial. The protests were the largest in Hong Kong since the 2014 Umbrella Movement.

The protests were marked by a series of large-scale demonstrations, often involving hundreds of thousands of people, and a variety of tactics, including sit-ins, roadblocks, and occupations of public spaces. The protesters demanded the full implementation of universal suffrage, an independent investigation into alleged police brutality, the release of arrested protesters, and the resignation of Chief Executive Carrie Lam.

The protests were met with a heavy-handed response from the Hong Kong government and police, which included the use of tear gas, rubber bullets, and water cannons. The protests were also met with counter-protests from pro-Beijing groups, and there were several instances of violence between protesters and counter-protesters.

The protests were a major test for the Hong Kong government and police, and they raised concerns about the erosion of civil liberties in Hong Kong. The protests also had a significant impact on Hong Kong's economy and tourism industry.

The protests eventually subsided in early 2020, but the underlying issues that sparked them remain unresolved. The protests were a watershed moment in Hong Kong's history, and they continue to have a profound impact on the city's politics and society.

## Background

The protests were sparked by the now-withdrawn extradition bill, which would have allowed the transfer of fugitives to mainland China for trial. The bill was seen as a threat to Hong Kong's autonomy and judicial independence.

The protests were also fueled by a variety of other grievances, including:

* The erosion of civil liberties in Hong Kong.

* The perceived corruption of the Hong Kong government.

* The high cost of living in Hong Kong.

* The lack of affordable housing.

* The lack of democracy in Hong Kong.

## Timeline

The protests began in late 2019 and continued into 2020. The timeline of the protests is as follows:

* June 9, 2019: Hundreds of thousands of people marched in Hong Kong to protest against the extradition bill.

* June 12, 2019: Police used tear gas and rubber bullets to disperse protesters in Hong Kong.

* June 16, 2019: The Hong Kong government suspended the extradition bill.

* August 31, 2019: The Hong Kong government formally withdrew the extradition bill.

* September 29, 2019: The Hong Kong government declared the protests to be illegal.

* October 1, 2019: The Hong Kong government announced a series of measures to address the protesters' demands.

* November 18, 2019: The Hong Kong government announced that it would not be implementing universal suffrage.

* December 8, 2019: The Hong Kong government announced that it would be banning face masks.

* January 1, 2020: The Hong Kong government implemented the ban on face masks.

* February 24, 2020: The Hong Kong government announced that it would be banning all public gatherings of more than four people.

* March 29, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until April 23.

* April 23, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until May 7.

* May 7, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until May 21.

* May 21, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until June 4.

* June 4, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until June 18.

* June 18, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until July 2.

* July 2, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until July 16.

* July 16, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until July 30.

* July 30, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until August 13.

* August 13, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until August 27.

* August 27, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until September 10.

* September 10, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until September 24.

* September 24, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until October 8.

* October 8, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until October 22.

* October 22, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until November 5.

* November 5, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until November 19.

* November 19, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until December 3.

* December 3, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until December 17.

* December 17, 2020: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until January 1, 2021.

* January 1, 2021: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until January 15.

* January 15, 2021: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until January 29.

* January 29, 2021: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until February 12.

* February 12, 2021: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until February 26.

* February 26, 2021: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until March 12.

* March 12, 2021: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until March 26.

* March 26, 2021: The Hong Kong government extended the ban on public gatherings until April 9.

# List of European tornadoes in 2024

This page documents all tornadoes confirmed by the European Severe Storms Laboratory or various governments or universities across Europe during 2024. Unlike the United States, the original Fujita Scale, the TORRO scale, and the International Fujita Scale are primarily used to rank tornadoes across the continent.

* Note: One tornado has been officially confirmed but is yet to receive a rating.

According to the European Severe Storms Laboratory, there have been 27 confirmed tornadoes in Europe in 2024, resulting in 0 fatalities and 1 injury. Note that non-damaging waterspouts are included in this number but not elsewhere.

# 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests

On 12 November, the Hong Kong government announced that it would ban face masks in public assemblies, citing the need to maintain public order. The ban was enacted on 4 October 2019, and was the first time the colonial-era Emergency Regulations Ordinance had been invoked since 1967. The ban was challenged in court by the Civil Rights Observer, a local human rights group. On 18 November, the High Court ruled that the ban was unconstitutional, and the government appealed the decision. On 29 November, the Court of Appeal overturned the High Court's ruling, upholding the ban. The government's decision to invoke the Emergency Regulations Ordinance was widely criticised by pro-democracy figures and international human rights groups, who argued that it was an excessive and disproportionate response to the protests.

## 2020

On 1 January 2020, thousands of protesters gathered in Victoria Park for the annual New Year's Day march, which was organised by the Civil Human Rights Front. The march was the first major protest of the year, and it was attended by a diverse range of people, including families with young children. The protesters called for the full implementation of the five demands, and they also expressed their opposition to the government's decision to invoke the Emergency Regulations Ordinance.

On 1 February, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be withdrawing the extradition bill. The announcement was made by Chief Executive Carrie Lam, who also apologised for the "mistakes" that had been made in handling the protests. The withdrawal of the extradition bill was one of the five demands of the protesters, and it was seen as a major victory for the pro-democracy movement.

Despite the withdrawal of the extradition bill, the protests continued. On 8 February, hundreds of thousands of people marched through the streets of Hong Kong to demand the full implementation of the five demands. The march was organised by the Civil Human Rights Front, and it was the largest protest of the year.

On 12 February, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be implementing a new round of measures to address the protesters' demands. The measures included the establishment of an independent commission of inquiry into the protests, the release of all arrested protesters, and the withdrawal of the mask ban. The government's announcement was welcomed by some protesters, but it was also met with scepticism, as many protesters felt that the measures did not go far enough.

# 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests

On 12 November, the Hong Kong government announced that it would ban face masks in public assemblies, citing the need to maintain public order. The ban was met with widespread criticism, with the opposition and legal experts arguing that it was unconstitutional and would only escalate the conflict. The ban was challenged in court, and on 18 November, the High Court ruled that the ban was unconstitutional, stating that it violated the right to freedom of expression. The government appealed the decision, and on 29 November, the Court of Appeal upheld the High Court's ruling, effectively reinstating the ban.

## 2020

On 1 January 2020, thousands of protesters gathered in Victoria Park to mark the 22nd anniversary of Hong Kong's handover from British to Chinese rule. The event was peaceful, with protesters calling for democracy and an end to police brutality.

On 12 January, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be introducing a new national security law, which would criminalize acts of secession, subversion, terrorism, and collusion with foreign forces. The law was met with widespread opposition, with critics arguing that it would erode Hong Kong's autonomy and freedom.

On 1 February, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be postponing the legislative elections, which were scheduled for September 2020, citing the need to prevent the spread of the coronavirus. The decision was met with widespread criticism, with opposition figures and pro-democracy activists accusing the government of using the pandemic as an excuse to delay the elections and maintain its grip on power.

On 30 May, the Hong Kong government passed the national security law, which criminalized acts of secession, subversion, terrorism, and collusion with foreign forces. The law was met with widespread condemnation, with critics arguing that it would stifle dissent and freedom of expression.

On 1 July, the Hong Kong government celebrated the 23rd anniversary of the handover from British to Chinese rule. The celebrations were muted, with many residents expressing their dissatisfaction with the government's handling of the protests and the new national security law.

On 12 July, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be introducing a new electoral reform package, which would limit the number of pro-democracy candidates in elections and give the government more control over the selection of candidates. The reform package was met with widespread opposition, with pro-democracy activists and opposition figures arguing that it would further erode Hong Kong's democracy.

On 1 August, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be banning the display of the British flag in public, citing the need to maintain national security. The ban was met with widespread criticism, with critics arguing that it was an attack on Hong Kong's history and culture.

On 1 September, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be introducing a new law that would criminalize disrespect for the Chinese national anthem. The law was met with widespread opposition, with critics arguing that it would stifle freedom of expression.

On 1 October, the Hong Kong government celebrated the 24th anniversary of the handover from British to Chinese rule. The celebrations were again muted, with many residents expressing their dissatisfaction with the government's handling of the protests and the new national security law.

On 1 November, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be introducing a new law that would criminalize the possession of pro-independence materials. The law was met with widespread opposition, with critics arguing that it would stifle freedom of expression.

On 1 December, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be introducing a new law that would criminalize the display of the pro-democracy flag. The law was met with widespread opposition, with critics arguing that it would stifle freedom of expression.

On 1 January 2021, the Hong Kong government celebrated the 25th anniversary of the handover from British to Chinese rule. The celebrations were again muted, with many residents expressing their dissatisfaction with the government's handling of the protests and the new national security law.

## Aftermath

The 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests had a profound impact on Hong Kong and its relationship with China. The protests were a watershed moment in Hong Kong's history, and they had far-reaching consequences for the city's future.

One of the most immediate consequences of the protests was the passage of the national security law. The law criminalized acts of secession, subversion, terrorism, and collusion with foreign forces, and it was seen as a major blow to Hong Kong's autonomy and freedom.

The protests also had a chilling effect on freedom of expression in Hong Kong. The government's crackdown on dissent, including the banning of face masks, the postponement of elections, and the introduction of new laws criminalizing disrespect for the national anthem and the possession of pro-independence materials, was seen as a further erosion of Hong Kong's freedoms.

The protests also had a significant impact on Hong Kong's economy. The tourism industry, which had been a major source of revenue for the city, was severely impacted by the protests. Businesses were also affected by the protests, with many companies either closing down or relocating to other parts of the world.

The protests also had a profound impact on Hong Kong's politics. The pro-democracy camp was weakened by the protests, and the government was able to consolidate its power. The opposition was marginalized, and the government was able to push through its agenda without much opposition.

The 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests were a complex and multifaceted event that had a profound impact on Hong Kong and its relationship with China. They were a testament to the resilience of the people of Hong Kong and their commitment to democracy and freedom. However, the protests also highlighted the fragility of Hong Kong's autonomy and the challenges that the city faces in the years ahead.

# 2019â€“202s

The 2019â€“20s were a series of protests in the United States against the Trump administration's family separation policy. The protests were organized by the group ShutDownDC, which was founded in 2018. The protests were held in Washington, D.C., and other cities across the United States.

## Background

The protests were in response to the Trump administration's family separation policy, which separated children from their families at the U.S.-Mexico border. The policy was implemented in April 2018 and was widely condemned by the public and by human rights groups.

The protests were also in response to the Trump administration's decision to end the policy in June 2018. The protests were held to demand that the Trump administration reunite the families that had been separated and to end the policy permanently.

## Protests

The protests were held on various dates from September 2019 to January 2020. The protests were typically held on weekends and were attended by thousands of people.

The protests were peaceful and were marked by a variety of activities, including speeches, music, and art. The protesters also held signs and chanted slogans such as "Families belong together" and "No human is illegal."

The protests were covered by a variety of media outlets, including CNN, Fox News, and The New York Times.

## Impact

The protests had a significant impact on public opinion and on the Trump administration's family separation policy. The protests helped to raise awareness of the issue and to put pressure on the Trump administration to reunite the families that had been separated.

The protests also led to a number of changes in the Trump administration's policy. In November 2019, the Trump administration announced that it would end its policy of separating families at the border. The administration also announced that it would reunite the families that had been separated.

The protests were a success in achieving their goals. They helped to raise awareness of the issue of family separation and they put pressure on the Trump administration to end the policy.

## Legacy

The 2019â€“20s protests are an important part of the history of the Trump administration and of the fight for immigrant rights in the United States. They are a reminder of the power of peaceful protest and of the importance of standing up for what is right.

# 2019â€“2020 United States blizzard

The 2019â€“2020 United States blizzard was a major winter storm that affected the United States from January 28 to February 1, 2020. The storm produced heavy snow, ice, and strong winds across the Midwest and the Great Lakes region, with blizzard conditions in parts of Minnesota and Wisconsin. The storm was the first major winter storm of the 2019â€“2020 North American winter.

## Meteorological history

On January 27, the Storm Prediction Center (SPC) outlined a slight risk for severe weather across portions of the Southern Plains, with a marginal risk for severe weather extending northward into the Upper Midwest. The next day, the SPC expanded the slight risk northward into Iowa and Minnesota, with a marginal risk for severe weather extending into Wisconsin and Michigan. On January 29, the SPC expanded the slight risk northward into Wisconsin and Michigan, with a marginal risk for severe weather extending into Ontario.

## Preparations

On January 28, the National Weather Service (NWS) issued a winter storm watch for portions of the Upper Midwest. The next day, the NWS issued a blizzard warning for portions of Minnesota and Wisconsin, with winter storm warnings and winter weather advisories issued for other parts of the Upper Midwest and the Great Lakes region.

## Impact

The storm produced heavy snow, ice, and strong winds across the Midwest and the Great Lakes region, with blizzard conditions in parts of Minnesota and Wisconsin.

## Minnesota

In Minnesota, the storm dropped heavy snow across the state, with blizzard conditions reported in parts of the Twin Cities metropolitan area. The storm also produced strong winds, with gusts of up to 50 mph reported in the Twin Cities. The storm caused widespread power outages, with over 100,000 customers losing power at the height of the storm. The storm also caused numerous traffic accidents, with Interstate 94 closed for several hours due to a pileup.

## Wisconsin

In Wisconsin, the storm dropped heavy snow across the state, with blizzard conditions reported in parts of the state. The storm also produced strong winds, with gusts of up to 60 mph reported in Milwaukee. The storm caused widespread power outages, with over 100,000 customers losing power at the height of the storm. The storm also caused numerous traffic accidents, with Interstate 94 closed for several hours due to a pileup.

## Michigan

In Michigan, the storm dropped heavy snow across the state, with blizzard conditions reported in parts of the Upper Peninsula. The storm also produced strong winds, with gusts of up to 50 mph reported in the Upper Peninsula. The storm caused widespread power outages, with over 100,000 customers losing power at the height of the storm. The storm also caused numerous traffic accidents, with Interstate 75 closed for several hours due to a pileup.

## Illinois

In Illinois, the storm dropped heavy snow across the state, with blizzard conditions reported in parts of the state. The storm also produced strong winds, with gusts of up to 40 mph reported in Chicago. The storm caused widespread power outages, with over 100,000 customers losing power at the height of the storm. The storm also caused numerous traffic accidents, with Interstate 55 closed for several hours due to a pileup.

## Indiana

In Indiana, the storm dropped heavy snow across the state, with blizzard conditions reported in parts of the state. The storm also produced strong winds, with gusts of up to 30 mph reported in Indianapolis. The storm caused widespread power outages, with over 100,000 customers losing power at the height of the storm. The storm also caused numerous traffic accidents, with Interstate 65 closed for several hours due to a pileup.

## Ohio

In Ohio, the storm dropped heavy snow across the state, with blizzard conditions reported in parts of the state. The storm also produced strong winds, with gusts of up to 30 mph reported in Cleveland. The storm caused widespread power outages, with over 100,000 customers losing power at the height of the storm. The storm also caused numerous traffic accidents, with Interstate 71 closed for several hours due to a pileup.

## Pennsylvania

In Pennsylvania, the storm dropped heavy snow across the state, with blizzard conditions reported in parts of the state. The storm also produced strong winds, with gusts of up to 20 mph reported in Philadelphia. The storm caused widespread power outages, with over 100,000 customers losing power at the height of the storm. The storm also caused numerous traffic accidents, with Interstate 95 closed for several hours due to a pileup.

## New York

In New York, the storm dropped heavy snow across the state, with blizzard conditions reported in parts of the state. The storm also produced strong winds, with gusts of up to 20 mph reported in New York City. The storm caused widespread power outages, with over 100,000 customers losing power at the height of the storm. The storm also caused numerous traffic accidents, with Interstate 87 closed for several hours due to a pileup.

# 1981 NPSL Second Division

The 1981 National Professional Soccer League was the fourth season of the multi-racial South African National Professional Soccer League (NPSL). The league was a merger of the previous NPSL, which due to the country's apartheid policies were for black teams only, and the National Football League, for white teams only. 

It was won by Kaizer Chiefs, who defeated defending champions by one point. Chiefs also won the Mainstay Cup, the BP Top 8 and the Sales House Champion of Champions. The 1981 Chiefs side is regarded on of the best in the country's history.

## References

# 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests

On 12 November, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be suspending the extradition bill. The announcement was made after a meeting of the Executive Council, the city's de facto cabinet. The government also announced that it would be setting up a commission to look into the root causes of the protests. The announcement was met with scepticism by protesters, who demanded a full withdrawal of the bill.

## 13 November

On 13 November, the day after the government announced that it would be suspending the extradition bill, hundreds of thousands of protesters took to the streets in a march in the Causeway Bay and Wan Chai districts. The march was organised by the Civil Human Rights Front. The march was the largest protest since the 1 October march.

## 18 November

On 18 November, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be withdrawing the extradition bill. The announcement was made by Chief Executive Carrie Lam at a press conference. The announcement was met with scepticism by protesters, who demanded the resignation of Carrie Lam and the fulfillment of their other demands.

## 24 November

On 24 November, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be setting up a commission of inquiry to investigate the root causes of the protests. The commission will be headed by retired judge, Andrew Li. The announcement was met with scepticism by protesters, who demanded the resignation of Carrie Lam and the fulfillment of their other demands.

## 29 November

On 29 November, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be setting up a task force to look into police conduct during the protests. The task force will be headed by retired judge, Michael Lunn. The announcement was met with scepticism by protesters, who demanded the resignation of Carrie Lam and the fulfillment of their other demands.

## 1 December

On 1 December, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be setting up a committee to look into the issue of police accountability. The committee will be headed by retired judge, Patrick Li. The announcement was met with scepticism by protesters, who demanded the resignation of Carrie Lam and the fulfillment of their other demands.

## 8 December

On 8 December, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be setting up a committee to look into the issue of political reform. The committee will be headed by retired judge, Albert Wong. The announcement was met with scepticism by protesters, who demanded the resignation of Carrie Lam and the fulfillment of their other demands.

## 15 December

On 15 December, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be setting up a committee to look into the issue of education reform. The committee will be headed by retired judge, Andrew Cheung. The announcement was met with scepticism by protesters, who demanded the resignation of Carrie Lam and the fulfillment of their other demands.

# 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests

On 12 November, the Hong Kong government announced that it would ban face masks in public assemblies, citing the need to maintain public order. The ban was met with widespread criticism from pro-democracy figures and legal experts, who argued that it was unconstitutional and would only escalate the conflict. The ban was challenged in court, and on 18 November, the High Court ruled that the ban was unconstitutional and therefore invalid. The government appealed the decision, and on 29 November, the Court of Appeal upheld the High Court's ruling.

## 2020

On 1 January 2020, thousands of protesters gathered in Victoria Park to mark the 22nd anniversary of Hong Kong's handover from British to Chinese rule. The event was peaceful, with protesters calling for democracy and an end to police brutality.

On 1 February, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be withdrawing the extradition bill that had sparked the protests. The announcement was met with mixed reactions, with some protesters calling it a victory and others demanding further concessions from the government.

On 8 February, a large pro-democracy rally was held in Hong Kong, with protesters calling for the full withdrawal of the extradition bill, an independent investigation into police conduct, and the release of all arrested protesters. The rally was peaceful, with an estimated 200,000 people in attendance.

On 12 February, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be implementing a new national security law that would criminalize acts of secession, subversion, terrorism, and collusion with foreign forces. The announcement was met with widespread opposition from pro-democracy figures and legal experts, who argued that the law would erode Hong Kong's autonomy and freedom.

On 27 February, a large pro-democracy rally was held in Hong Kong, with protesters calling for the full withdrawal of the national security law and the release of all arrested protesters. The rally was peaceful, with an estimated 250,000 people in attendance.

On 1 March, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be implementing a new electoral system that would limit the number of pro-democracy candidates in elections. The announcement was met with widespread opposition from pro-democracy figures and legal experts, who argued that the new system would undermine democracy in Hong Kong.

On 15 March, a large pro-democracy rally was held in Hong Kong, with protesters calling for the full withdrawal of the new electoral system and the release of all arrested protesters. The rally was peaceful, with an estimated 200,000 people in attendance.

On 27 March, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be implementing a new law that would criminalize disrespect for the Chinese national anthem. The announcement was met with widespread opposition from pro-democracy figures and legal experts, who argued that the law would further erode Hong Kong's autonomy and freedom.

On 1 April, a large pro-democracy rally was held in Hong Kong, with protesters calling for the full withdrawal of the new national anthem law and the release of all arrested protesters. The rally was peaceful, with an estimated 250,000 people in attendance.

On 15 April, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be implementing a new law that would criminalize the display of the British flag in public. The announcement was met with widespread opposition from pro-democracy figures and legal experts, who argued that the law would further erode Hong Kong's autonomy and freedom.

On 29 April, a large pro-democracy rally was held in Hong Kong, with protesters calling for the full withdrawal of the new national anthem law and the release of all arrested protesters. The rally was peaceful, with an estimated 200,000 people in attendance.

On 13 May, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be implementing a new law that would criminalize the display of the American flag in public. The announcement was met with widespread opposition from pro-democracy figures and legal experts, who argued that the law would further erode Hong Kong's autonomy and freedom.

On 27 May, a large pro-democracy rally was held in Hong Kong, with protesters calling for the full withdrawal of the new national anthem law and the release of all arrested protesters. The rally was peaceful, with an estimated 250,000 people in attendance.

On 10 June, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be implementing a new law that would criminalize the display of the European Union flag in public. The announcement was met with widespread opposition from pro-democracy figures and legal experts, who argued that the law would further erode Hong Kong's autonomy and freedom.

On 24 June, a large pro-democracy rally was held in Hong Kong, with protesters calling for the full withdrawal of the new national anthem law and the release of all arrested protesters. The rally was peaceful, with an estimated 200,000 people in attendance.

On 8 July, the Hong Kong government announced that it would be implementing a new law that would criminalize the display of the Australian flag in public. The announcement was met with widespread opposition from pro-democracy figures and legal experts, who argued that the law would further erode Hong Kong's autonomy and freedom.

# 2019â€“2020 United Kingdom budget

The 2019â€“2020 United Kingdom budget was delivered by the Chancellor of the Exchequer, Sajid Javid, on 6 November 2019. It was the first budget of the second premiership of Boris Johnson and the first budget of the 58th Parliament of the United Kingdom.

## Background

The budget was delivered on 6 November 2019, the first budget of the second premiership of Boris Johnson and the first budget of the 58th Parliament of the United Kingdom. It was the first budget to be delivered by Sajid Javid, who had been appointed Chancellor of the Exchequer in July 2019.

## Economic forecasts

The Office for Budget Responsibility (OBR) forecast that the UK economy would grow by 1.1% in 2019, 1.3% in 2020, 1.5% in 2021, 1.6% in 2022 and 1.7% in 2023.

## Tax

The budget included a number of tax changes:

* The National Insurance threshold was raised to Â£9,500, the same as the income tax personal allowance.

* The main rate of corporation tax was cut from 19% to 17% from April 2020.

* The small profits rate of corporation tax was cut from 14% to 12.5% from April 2020.

* The rate of stamp duty on properties worth between Â£125,000 and Â£250,000 was cut from 5% to 2%.

* The rate of stamp duty on properties worth between Â£250,000 and Â£925,000 was cut from 10% to 5%.

* The rate of stamp duty on properties worth more than Â£925,000 was cut from 12% to 10%.

* The rate of stamp duty on commercial properties was cut from 3% to 2%.

* The rate of business rates was cut from 51.2p to 49.9p in the pound.

* The rate of air passenger duty on domestic flights was cut from Â£13 to Â£8.

* The rate of air passenger duty on long-haul flights was cut from Â£71 to Â£60.

* The rate of air passenger duty on short-haul flights was cut from Â£13 to Â£3.

* The rate of air passenger duty on medium-haul flights was cut from Â£26 to Â£13.

* The rate of fuel duty was frozen for the 10th consecutive year.

* The rate of alcohol duty was frozen for the 7th consecutive year.

* The rate of beer duty was cut by 2p per pint.

* The rate of cider duty was cut by 2p per pint.

* The rate of wine duty was cut by 2p per bottle.

* The rate of spirits duty was cut by 37p per bottle.

* The rate of tobacco duty was increased by 2%.

* The rate of vaping products duty was set at 20% of the retail price.

* The rate of sugar tax was increased from 18p to 24p per litre.

* The rate of plastic packaging tax was set at Â£200 per tonne.

* The rate of landfill tax was increased by Â£2 per tonne.

* The rate of climate change levy was increased by Â£1.80 per tonne.

* The rate of apprenticeship levy was cut from 0.5% to 0.3%.

* The rate of R&D tax credit was increased from 130% to 145%.

* The rate of R&D tax relief was increased from 110% to 130%.

* The rate of R&D tax relief for small businesses was increased from 110% to 130%.

* The rate of R&D tax relief for medium-sized businesses was increased from 110% to 130%.

* The rate of R&D tax relief for large businesses was increased from 110% to 130%.

* The rate of R&D tax relief for businesses in the creative industries was increased from 110% to 130%.

* The rate of R&D tax relief for businesses in the life sciences sector was increased from 110% to 130%.

* The rate of R&D tax relief for businesses in the digital technology sector was increased from 110% to 130%.

* The rate of R&D tax relief for businesses in the clean energy sector was increased from 110% to 130%.

* The rate of R&D tax relief for businesses in the space sector was increased from 110% to 130%.

# 2019â€“2020 Hong Kong protests

On 12 November, the Hong Kong government announced that it would ban face masks in public assemblies, with exceptions for those with medical needs. The ban was enacted on 4 October 2019, and was the first time the colonial-era Emergency Regulations Ordinance had been invoked since 1967. The ban was challenged in court by the Civil Human Rights Front, and on 18 November, the High Court ruled that the ban was unconstitutional. The government appealed the decision, and on 29 November, the Court of Final Appeal upheld the ban.

## 2020

On 1 January 2020, thousands of protesters gathered in Victoria Park to celebrate the new year and the movement's first anniversary. The event was peaceful, with protesters singing the Hong Kong national anthem and the pro-democracy anthem "Glory to Hong Kong".

# 1972 NASCAR Winston Cup Series

The 1972 NASCAR Winston Cup Series was the 24th season of the NASCAR Grand National Series. The season began on January 23, 1972, and ended on December 3, 1972. Richard Petty won his fourth championship, 27 points ahead of Bobby Allison.

## Schedule and results

The 1972 season included 48 individual races, although some tracks were run twice. Multiple races in the season were in combination with the Winston West Series.
